We denote by $f(N)$ the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$ (if $N$ has at least three proper divisors). The sequence is defined by $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$ with $a_1\in S$ where $S$ is the set of positive integers having at least three proper divisors.

We first characterize $f(N)$.

**Lemma 1.** For $N$ not divisible by $6$, we have $f(N)<N$.
*Proof.* If $N$ is odd, its smallest prime factor $p\ge3$, so the three largest proper divisors are at most $N/p$, $N/p^2$, $N/p^3$. Hence $f(N)\le N(1/p+1/p^2+1/p^3)\le N(1/3+1/9+1/27)=13N/27<N$. If $N$ is even but not divisible by $3$, write $N=2^\alpha M$ with $M$ odd and $3\nmid M$. The largest proper divisor is $N/2=2^{\alpha-1}M$. The second largest is at most $N/4$ (if $\alpha\ge2$) or $N/5$ (if $\alpha=1$ and $5\mid M$); the third largest is at most $N/5$ or $N/7$ etc. In any case we can bound $f(N)\le N/2+N/4+N/5=19N/20<N$. (For $\alpha=1$, the bound is even smaller.) Thus $f(N)<N$. ∎

**Lemma 2.** For $N$ divisible by $6$, we have $f(N)\ge N$, with equality iff $N/6$ is odd and not divisible by $5$.
*Proof.* Write $N=6k$. Consider the prime factorization. Since $N$ is even, the exponent of $2$ is at least $1$. We treat two cases.

- If $k$ is even, then $N$ is divisible by $12$. Then $N/2$, $N/3$, $N/4$ are proper divisors, and they are the three largest because $N/2>N/3>N/4$ and any other divisor is $\le N/5$ (since $N/3$ already uses the factor $3$, but $N/5$ might be integer if $5\mid N$; however $N/4 > N/5$ always). In fact $N/4$ is an integer and $N/4 > N/5$ if $5\mid N$, else $N/4$ is still larger than $N/5$. So the three largest are $N/2$, $N/3$, $N/4$. Their sum is $N(1/2+1/3+1/4)=13N/12>N$. Thus $f(N)>N$.

- If $k$ is odd, then $N$ is not divisible by $4$. Since $N=2\cdot3\cdot k$ with $k$ odd, the largest proper divisor is $N/2=3k$, the second largest is $N/3=2k$, and the third largest is the largest divisor of $N$ that is $<2k$. The divisors of $N$ are of the form $2^a3^b d$ where $a\in\{0,1\}$, $b\in[0,1]$, $d\mid k$ (because $k$ is odd and not divisible by $2$ or $3$? Actually $k$ may be divisible by $3$; but $N$ still has exactly one factor $2$, so $N/4$ is not integer). The only divisors larger than $k$ are $2k$ and $3k$ (from $N/3$ and $N/2$). Also $N/5$ is integer if $5\mid k$, but $N/5=6k/5$, which is less than $2k$ because $6k/5<2k$ for $k>0$. So $N/5$ is less than $2k$. The third largest could be $k$ if there is no divisor between $k$ and $2k$. If $k$ is not divisible by $5$, then $N/5$ is not integer, and the only divisors in $(k,2k)$ are $2k$ and $3k$? Actually $2k$ and $3k$ are already the largest two. So $k$ is the third largest. Hence $f(N)=3k+2k+k=6k=N$. If $k$ is divisible by $5$, then $N/5=6k/5$ is a divisor. Since $k$ is odd and $5\mid k$, $6k/5$ is integer. Compare $6k/5$ with $k$: $6k/5 > k$ because $6/5>1$. Also $6k/5 < 2k$ (since $6/5<2$). Hence the third largest is $6k/5$, which is larger than $k$. Then $f(N)=3k+2k+6k/5=31k/5$. Let $k=5t$, then $f(N)=31t$. Since $N=6\cdot5t=30t$, we have $f(N)=31t>30t=N$. Thus $f(N)>N$.

Therefore $f(N)\ge N$ for all $N$ divisible by $6$, with equality only when $N/6$ is odd and not divisible by $5$. ∎

Now we determine all $a_1$ for which the sequence stays in $S$ forever.

**Case 1: $a_1$ is a fixed point.** By Lemma 2, the fixed points are exactly the numbers $N$ such that $N/6$ is odd and not divisible by $5$. For such $a_1$, we have $a_2=f(a_1)=a_1$, so the sequence is constant. Since $a_1$ itself has at least three proper divisors, all terms are in $S$. Hence every such $a_1$ works.

**Case 2: $a_1=72u$ with $u$ odd and $5\nmid u$.** Compute $f(72u)$. Since $72u$ is divisible by $12$, we are in the first subcase of Lemma 2: $a_1/6=12u$ is even. Then $f(72u)=13\cdot(72u/12)=13\cdot6u=78u$. Now $78u/6=13u$, and $13u$ is odd because $u$ is odd, and $5\nmid13u$ because $5\nmid u$ and $5\nmid13$. Thus $78u$ is a fixed point. Hence $a_2=78u$ is a fixed point, so $a_3=a_2$, and the sequence becomes constant. Therefore $a_1$ works.

**Case 3: $a_1$ is not a fixed point and $a_1\neq 72u$ with $u$ odd and $5\nmid u$.** We show that the sequence eventually leaves $S$.

- If $a_1$ is not a multiple of $6$, then by Lemma 1, $a_2=f(a_1)<a_1$, and $a_2\in S$. By induction, the sequence is strictly decreasing: $a_1>a_2>a_3>\cdots$. Since the positive integers are well-ordered, the sequence cannot decrease forever; it must eventually reach a number that is not in $S$, i.e., a prime or a prime square. Hence the condition fails.

- If $a_1$ is a multiple of $6$ but not a fixed point, then by Lemma 2, $a_2>a_1$. We examine the two possibilities for $a_1$:

   *Subcase 3a:* $a_1\equiv6\pmod{12}$ (i.e., $a_1/2$ odd). Then $a_1=30k$ with $k$ odd (since $a_1/6=5k$). By Lemma 2, $f(a_1)=31k$, which is not divisible by $2$ or $3$ (because $k$ is odd and $31k$ is odd, and $31k$ is not divisible by $3$). Hence $a_2=31k$ is not a multiple of $6$. Then by Lemma 1, $a_3=f(a_2)<a_2$. So we have $a_1<a_2$ and $a_3<a_2$. The subsequence $a_3,a_4,\ldots$ is strictly decreasing, so it must eventually leave $S$. Therefore the sequence does not stay in $S$ forever.

   *Subcase 3b:* $a_1\equiv0\pmod{12}$. Then $a_1=12t$ for some integer $t$. Then $a_2=f(a_1)=13t$. Now if $t$ is not a multiple of $6$, then $a_2$ is not a multiple of $6$, so by Lemma 1, $a_3<a_2$, and again we get a strictly decreasing subsequence from $a_3$ onward, which cannot stay in $S$ forever. If $t$ is a multiple of $6$, then $t=6u$, so $a_1=72u$, which is exactly the case we already handled (Case 2). But we are in Case 3 where $a_1$ is not of that form. Hence this subcase does not occur.

Thus in all remaining possibilities, the sequence eventually reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors, violating the requirement that every term has at least three proper divisors. Consequently, the only possible values of $a_1$ are those described in Cases 1 and 2.

Therefore, all possible initial values are the numbers $N$ for which either $N/6$ is odd and not divisible by $5$, or $N/72$ is odd and not divisible by $5$. In other words,
\[
\boxed{a_1\in\{6k\mid k\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid k\}\cup\{72u\mid u\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid u\}}.
